<span id="freeTextContainer7557998361692164839">To survive as a detective in 1950s communist Russia, you have to put your country above all. Anything less is tantamount to high treason.<br/><br/>When your superiors tell you to investigate a possible spy, you go and apprehend him/her without stopping to think about the accuracy of the accusation: "<em>Better let ten innocent men suffer than one spy escape.</em>"<br/><br/>If your subordinate thinks his son's tragic accident was in fact murder, you go and remind him of one of the fundaments of his society: "<em>There is no crim</em></span>
  <span id="freeText7557998361692164839" style="display:none">To survive as a detective in 1950s communist Russia, you have to put your country above all. Anything less is tantamount to high treason.<br><br>When your superiors tell you to investigate a possible spy, you go and apprehend him/her without stopping to think about the accuracy of the accusation: "<em>Better let ten innocent men suffer than one spy escape.</em>"<br><br>If your subordinate thinks his son's tragic accident was in fact murder, you go and remind him of one of the fundaments of his society: "<em>There is no crime</em>".<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445158417i/16609017._SX540_.png" alt="Child ballancing on snow covered train tracks" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So when he refuses to denounce his own wife as a traitor, MGB detective Leo Demidov knows that they'll both be executed. Stalin's sudden death however, grants them reprieve and an exile into the remote town of Voualsk, where they'll at least have one another. But then his wife Raisa shocks him, by suddenly confessing that she had married Leo out of fear, and in fact hates him.<br><br>A "routine" arrest in the case of a dead child, whose circumstances seem eerily familiar, gives Leo a new purpose. He decides to find the actual culprit, and not just allow the militia to pin it on an unfortunate scapegoat.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445158417i/16609019._SX540_.png" alt="Crime scene in the woods" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Having previously watched <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Uia6y9SRsj4" title="External link to movie trailer" rel="nofollow noopener">the movie</a>, I was not expecting to like the book so much.<br>The basic story about a disgraced MGB agent hunting a serial child murderer remains the same, but life in the cut-throat world of communist Russia, as well as Leo's past <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(and as such his connection to the killer) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> are missing.<br><br>I especially liked Leo's character development. <br>In the movie, there is Tom Hardy who's running around and brooding handsomely, while the rest of the world is busy hating him. <br>In the book, we see Leo's life as a dedicated MGB agent, who would not hesitate to resort to drugs in order to keep doing his job, even when this comes with severe memory loss.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445158417i/16609021._SX540_.png" alt="Leo looking at a rain-splattered window" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It was also quite fascinating to read about Leo's inner turmoil, when it came down to actually <em>believing</em> the communist propaganda that he enforces. At times, it was almost heart-breaking to see him attempt to brainwash himself, by repeatedly memorizing communist slogans, when the methamphetamine-induced memory loss manifested itself.<br><br>And most of all, Leo's past is what left quite an impression on me. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Despite the fact that the novel actually starts with a scene from his childhood, the reader doesn't find its relevance until after having gone through more than half of the book. The chilling conclusion of this first chapter is, nevertheless, an excellent way of catching the reader's attention. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br><strong>Score: 5/5 stars</strong><br><br>The first 5 star book this year. <br>Admittedly, I cannot vouch for the accuracy of the information, when it comes to detailing life in 1950s Russia, but the manner in which it is described is definitely effective. I could barely put the book down, and even then I kept counting the hours I could pick it up again to continue.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_24792137" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24792137" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1177534-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593697601p2/1177534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24792137?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1177534-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780446402385" href="/book/show/3360518-child-44">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=read-in-2008">read-in-2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24792137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6734774136645626761">Child 44 is a novel that's hard to figure out where to place on the bookshelf. It's a political thriller, a murder mystery and a horror story all in one. Combining those elements alone would have been enough, but first-time novelist Tom Rob Smith takes is further, setting his story around the time of the death of Stalin in the former Soviet Union. Smith recreates the atmosphere of paranoia, doubt and suspicion of the time and place with ease, adding an extra layer of tension to his story.<br/><br/>On the </span>
  <span id="freeText6734774136645626761" style="display:none">Child 44 is a novel that's hard to figure out where to place on the bookshelf. It's a political thriller, a murder mystery and a horror story all in one. Combining those elements alone would have been enough, but first-time novelist Tom Rob Smith takes is further, setting his story around the time of the death of Stalin in the former Soviet Union. Smith recreates the atmosphere of paranoia, doubt and suspicion of the time and place with ease, adding an extra layer of tension to his story.<br /><br />On the surface, a story about someone serial killing small children would be brutal and horrifying enough. But add in that under the Soviet system that the government was creating a worker's paradise in which crime can't and shouldn't exist and you get a further twist on the serial hunter genre. If there is no crime, then surely such brutal crimes can't be occurring, adding to the complications as our hero, Leo, investigates the killings.<br /><br />And it's not that Leo doesn't have more than a few obstacles in his way. He starts the novel as high-ranking official in the MGB, a rising star whose job it is to find all those who are oppose Mother Russia. Along the way, he's had successes and has made enemies, including one who sets him up. Leo is forced to choose between betraying his wife, who is accused of being a spy, to save his own skin or siding with her. Leo makes the choice and his entire world shatters. He's humilated publically, his family's status is removed and he finds out that his romantic notions of his marriage are an illusion. Demoted and sent to a remote region as the lowest of the low in the police, Leo comes across evidence that an earlier crime he investigated may tie into killings in the region. Leo risks everything to investigate the crime.<br /><br />Smith's novel is compelling and page-turning. Smith is an aspiring screen-writer and there are moments in the story that he paints scenes in a movie-like way. But to dismiss the novel as one of those "ready to be adapted for a movie" would be a mistake. Smith's ability to re-create Russia in the time of Stalin is compelling as are the characters of Leo and his family. There are horrors here and not just those committed by the serial killer. Leo is certainly no innocent victim and yet, you will find yourself rooting for him as the pages go on. (He's not the most likeable character when you first meet him).<br /><br />A great debut by Smith and I can see why this has already been optioned for a big-screen adaption. But I will recommend that this is one of those cases where you will definitely want to read the book first.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6734774136645626761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Abram " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59693811-abram"><img alt="Abram " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571161273p2/59693811.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2363151244?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abram " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abram " href="/user/show/59693811-abram">Abram </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847391599" href="/book/show/4040988-child-44">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59693811-abram?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59693811-abram?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2363151244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13294205243677274020">My Rating: 4.2/5<br/><br/>“To stand up for someone was to stitch your fate into the lining of theirs.” <br/>“There's nothing more stubborn than a fact. That is why you hate them so much. They offend you.” <br/>“Trust but check. Check on those we trust.” <br/><br/>This is a very unique novel which reminded me again and again of George Orwell's 1984 but this one, I loved. I didn't really like the characters (at the end of the book, they are likable) but they all are certainly understandable and interesting. Tom Rob Smith is de</span>
  <span id="freeText13294205243677274020" style="display:none">My Rating: 4.2/5<br /><br />“To stand up for someone was to stitch your fate into the lining of theirs.” <br />“There's nothing more stubborn than a fact. That is why you hate them so much. They offend you.” <br />“Trust but check. Check on those we trust.” <br /><br />This is a very unique novel which reminded me again and again of George Orwell's 1984 but this one, I loved. I didn't really like the characters (at the end of the book, they are likable) but they all are certainly understandable and interesting. Tom Rob Smith is definitely a gifted writer who has a great way of telling his stories. It could be his non usage of quotation marks or the shifting narration, but it kept me guessing and hooked. The way the story went up and down, with the sudden turns made me unable to put the book down. I am going to read the next two books in the trilogy and I hope they would be as good as this. I would certainly recommend this book to everyone who loves thriller mystery novels with politics and philosophical and sociological traits. <br />Thank you for reading this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13294205243677274020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The hero police detective Leo Demidov desperately searches for a murderer (who came to be known as the Rostov Ripper, found to have committed 52 murders of children up and down a railway line), all while Demidov operates one step ahead of the breath-quickening bear jaws of the KGB that seeks to enforce as truth the propagand</span>
  <span id="freeText3607762028156973497" style="display:none"><b>Desperately Hunting Child Killer With Bear's Hot Breath on Da Bum</b><br /><br />4.5<br /><br />An intense thriller that places the reader dead center into the gloom and dread of Stalin's Soviet Union. The hero police detective Leo Demidov desperately searches for a murderer (who came to be known as the Rostov Ripper, found to have committed 52 murders of children up and down a railway line), all while Demidov operates one step ahead of the breath-quickening bear jaws of the KGB that seeks to enforce as truth the propaganda that there are NO murders committed in a utopian communist society. <br /><br />This is a well-written, brilliantly plotted police procedural, embodying the horrors of life for, and trying to save the lives of children by, <i>the good guys</i> under the awful, oppressive, murderous Stalin regime.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_471192597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/471192597" />
    <a title="Tania" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6450088-tania"><img alt="Tania" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603638299p2/6450088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/471192597?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tania" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tania" href="/user/show/6450088-tania">Tania</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tania by: </span>
      riaan
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=best">best</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer471192597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14002191793630100146"><blockquote>For decades no one had taken action according to what they believed was right or wrong but by what they thought would please the leader.</blockquote><br/>I thought this book was riveting. I couldn't believe it was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/981834.Tom_Rob_Smith' title='Tom Rob Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tom Rob Smith</a>'s debut novel. I especially appreciated how the he combined two genre's (historical fiction and mystery)seamlessly. He painted such an incredibly vivid picture of Stalin's Soviet Union in the 1950's you could really feel the terror, fear and cruelty of a whole country. Friends and family d</span>
  <span id="freeText14002191793630100146" style="display:none"><blockquote>For decades no one had taken action according to what they believed was right or wrong but by what they thought would please the leader.</blockquote><br />I thought this book was riveting. I couldn't believe it was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/981834.Tom_Rob_Smith" title="Tom Rob Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Tom Rob Smith</a>'s debut novel. I especially appreciated how the he combined two genre's (historical fiction and mystery)seamlessly. He painted such an incredibly vivid picture of Stalin's Soviet Union in the 1950's you could really feel the terror, fear and cruelty of a whole country. Friends and family denounced each other, to deflect any unwelcome attention from themselves. Once you fell under suspicion, there was no turning back. The government functioned on a presumption of guilt. As the author so aptly says - The headquarters of the MGB <i>was an assembly line of guilt.</i><br /><br />Another change for me in Child 44 was that the protagonist was an MGB agent. Most of the other war/genocide books I've read was presented from the viewpoint of the suppressed/victims. The author even delves into the various motivations why different people would fulfill these brutal roles. <i>Was the difference merely that Vasili was senselessly cruel while he'd been idealistically cruel? One was an empty, indifferent cruelty while the other was a principles, pretentious cruelty which thought of itself as reasonable and necessary. But in real terms, in destructive terms, there was little to separate the two men.</i><br /><br />All of the above is just the background for the mystery element of the novel. As the state decreed that there was no crime, this was the ideal environment for a murderer. The story itself is loosely based on the serial killer, Andrei Chikatilo. It is fast-paced and shocking, I found myself gasping out loud quite a few times. Some of the scenes are very upsetting and this is not for sensitive readers.<br /><br />My only negative is that the ending was a bit too well wrapped up.<br /><br />The Story: Officer Leo Demidov, an idealistic war hero, believes he's building a perfect society. But after witnessing the interrogation of an innocent man, his loyalty begins to waver, and when ordered to investigate his own wife Leo is forced to choose where his heart truly lies. He must risk everything to find a criminal that the State won't admit even exists. On the run, Leo soon discovers the danger isn't from the killer he is trying to catch, but from the country he is trying to protect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14002191793630100146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathrine ☯️ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathrine ☯️ " href="/user/show/22600410-cathrine">Cathrine ☯️ </a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1474705391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17661636249274069359"><b>4★</b><br/><br/>I was in the mood for a thriller and picked this up based on GR friends reviews. It did not disappoint. A page turner perfect for a couple of cold rainy days with nothing interesting on TV. The author takes us back to the post-war 1950’s reworking the true story of a Russian serial killer. First of a trilogy that did not leave the reader hanging.Yay! It did not have quite the punch of I Am Pilgrim which I read earlier in the year, but it was not as far fetched either, more authentic with great</span>
  <span id="freeText17661636249274069359" style="display:none"><b>4★</b><br /><br />I was in the mood for a thriller and picked this up based on GR friends reviews. It did not disappoint. A page turner perfect for a couple of cold rainy days with nothing interesting on TV. The author takes us back to the post-war 1950’s reworking the true story of a Russian serial killer. First of a trilogy that did not leave the reader hanging.Yay! It did not have quite the punch of I Am Pilgrim which I read earlier in the year, but it was not as far fetched either, more authentic with great pacing. You know what I’m talking about. This is not Mission Impossible whatever with guys impossibly hanging off of skyscrapers. Too bad they messed up the movie version, apparently, but I’ll give it a try anyway just to see the characters brought to life (or death). Additionally, I was able to drink my wine and follow along with no problem keeping the story straight or character mix-ups. A straight forward GoodRead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17661636249274069359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1474705391">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1793954986" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793954986" />
    <a title="Andrew Smith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5885760-andrew-smith"><img alt="Andrew Smith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605605555p2/5885760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793954986?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Smith" href="/user/show/5885760-andrew-smith">Andrew Smith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781600241598" href="/book/show/2161735.Child_44">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1793954986&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793954986" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2225661264783543517">This one has been on my list for ages. I’d read some excellent reviews, way back. I recalled reading that it's set in Russia in the 50’s and that it’s a crime novel,but I couldn’t remember much else. I counted this as a good thing as I really like to know as little as possible about a book before I launch into it.<br/><br/>It opens with a scene in which a young boy is captured by a man and dragged away to an unknown fate, this during a time of famine. It’s a disturbing start. Then, without any further ref</span>
  <span id="freeText2225661264783543517" style="display:none">This one has been on my list for ages. I’d read some excellent reviews, way back. I recalled reading that it's set in Russia in the 50’s and that it’s a crime novel,but I couldn’t remember much else. I counted this as a good thing as I really like to know as little as possible about a book before I launch into it.<br /><br />It opens with a scene in which a young boy is captured by a man and dragged away to an unknown fate, this during a time of famine. It’s a disturbing start. Then, without any further reference to this scene the narrative jumps forward twenty years. We’re introduced to Leo, a senior apparatchik in the secret police – the M.G.B. His role is to root out anyone suspected of questioning the Soviet regime. This behaviour would not be tolerated and that's ok with Leo – an ex-soldier – who is nothing if not patriotic. <br /><br />Leo is sent to the home of a colleague whose son has been killed. The man is convinced it’s a case of murder but the state has already declared the death to be an accident, the result of the boy wondering too close to a railway line and being struck by a moving train. It’s made clear that taking a position of disagreement with this view is not an option, the matter is soon closed down – an accident it is.<br /><br />This task, it turns out, was a diversion from Leo’s main current duty - that of monitoring a local vet who is suspected of using his contact with a local Western embassy to engage in collusion with ‘the enemy’. But the vet has now gone on the run and Leo must track him down or face the serious consequences of not arresting him when he had the chance. During this operation it becomes clear that a junior officer covets Leo’s role and is prepared to go to some lengths to ensure he achieves his aim.<br /><br />There is a point in this book at which Leo starts to have doubts about the the actions of the state and this coincides with a conviction that maybe the death of his colleague's son was murder after all. To say any more about the complex plot would be to give too much away, but it’s fair to say that there is a pretty even mix of focus on Leo’s attempts to chase down a hideous individual, who really needs to be caught and dealt with in the harshest possible way, and on the state's refusal to accept that the criminal exists at all. The view taken by senior officials is simply that violent crime is a function of capitalist depravity and that in the worker’s paradise that is the Soviet Union such a beast cannot exist. Besides, here only political-thought crimes matter.<br /><br />To say Leo has a lot on his plate would be to understate matters: to pour salt on the wound, his wife has declared that she doesn’t love him and only married him as she was scared of him. Ouch! Will he be able to sort out this tangled assemblage of challenges and come out smiling? Oh, I forgot – there’s precious little smiling to be had here. Its not that it’s prohibited, just that there’s not much to smile about!<br /><br />Set primarily in the months just before and shortly after the death of Stalin, this book really does throw the door open on the political and social issues of the time. It’s also an excellent crime thriller. If I have a minor criticism it would be that the ending, tying up all the loose threads, is just too neat and tidy – very Hollywood. But that aside I do believe that this is a great story which, for the most part, is very well told indeed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2225661264783543517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_961103696" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/961103696" />
    <a title="Mish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4045725-mish"><img alt="Mish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528852904p2/4045725.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/961103696?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mish" href="/user/show/4045725-mish">Mish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=favourite">favourite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4045725-mish?shelf=2000">2000</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer961103696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15489474017454495329">The body of a little boy was discovered along the railway tracks in th city of Moscow, violently mutilated. Leo Demidov, a dedicated MGB officer was ordered by his superiors to tell the grieving family that their boy’s death was an unfortunate accident. The boy’s family were not convinced.<br/><br/>Soon after Leo’s loyalty to the MGB were tested. And as a result, he was demoted and sent off to work with the Militia in an industrial village called Voualsk where another body of a child was found. Without a </span>
  <span id="freeText15489474017454495329" style="display:none">The body of a little boy was discovered along the railway tracks in th city of Moscow, violently mutilated. Leo Demidov, a dedicated MGB officer was ordered by his superiors to tell the grieving family that their boy’s death was an unfortunate accident. The boy’s family were not convinced.<br /><br />Soon after Leo’s loyalty to the MGB were tested. And as a result, he was demoted and sent off to work with the Militia in an industrial village called Voualsk where another body of a child was found. Without a thorough investigation, the head of Voualsk’s Militia arrested a simple-minded young man for the murder (an easy target), and put it down to a one off incident. <br /><br />Leo is starting to think otherwise. There were too many similarities between in the 2 deaths to be a one off incident; the manner in which body was discovered were the same, including the injuries suffered. With the help of his wife and a few locals in connecting villages, Leo conducts his own investigation. And what he finds is more horrific then he imagined! A total of 44 deaths - all children, dumped along the railway track, stripped, cut, and laid out...all in the same manner. <br /><br />And total of 44 crimes covered up by their own government. <br /><br />Child 44 is set in 1953 under horrendous era of Stalin’s reign where they believe that crime doesn’t exists – hence the reason why the killer went undetected for so long. The crime in Child 44 is not the sole highlight in this book. The reader gets extensive insight into lives of Russians during this era; struggling to survive under the strict and unforgiving rules, and the lack of adequate housing and basic infrastructure. It’s also a fascinating portrayal of a society living in constant paranoia, stress and fear of a government should be protecting them and their children, not the killers. Paranoid that their own family, friends or neighbours can turn against them in a split second to save their own back from government threats and bullying. Fear to even speak in public or to be noticed, as anything they say or do could be misinterpreted as a comment or behaviour against Stalin. <br /><br />Leo Demidov is such an interesting, strong character. Not your average likable hero to begin with. He starts out as an officer who's responsible for the deaths of many innocent victims, and destroyed equally as many families along the way, because of his dedication and trust in the Stalin way. His wife hated him and to be honest I wasn’t too fond of him either – cold-hearted bastard to say the least! He did somewhat redeem himself as the story progress. I really liked witnessing this unexpected change in manner, and the growing relationship between Leo and his wife. <br /><br />I wanted to read this book so desperately before I saw movie adaption. And what a book it is! The plot was purely engrossing, suspenseful and brutally violent at times by the nature of the killings. A sense of danger and darkness looms heavily over this book as Leo and his wife risk their lives from sheer determination to catch the killer, while being watched and on the run from MGB. The wet, icy cold backdrop, merged with tension, drama, love and corruption. It really does have all. Terrific read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15489474017454495329" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19470614-lilo?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19470614-lilo?shelf=received-as-a-gift">received-as-a-gift</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19470614-lilo?shelf=soviet-union">soviet-union</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19470614-lilo?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19470614-lilo?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer682588079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7545560186289189720">I read "Child 44" in 2012. I don't remember enough of it to write an appropriate review. But I can say wholeheartedly that it is an absolute must-read for anyone who wants to get an idea of what it is like to live under a totalitarian regime.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1224093705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1224093705" />
    <a title="☮Karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1076079-karen"><img alt="☮Karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605826912p2/1076079.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1224093705?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="☮Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="☮Karen" href="/user/show/1076079-karen">☮Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=challenges">challenges</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=overdrive">overdrive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=500-plus-pages">500-plus-pages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1076079-karen?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1224093705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2268569642997657477">Three words: FAB - U - LOUS! So much to love in this multi-genre book--part historical fiction and cultural; part murder mystery/psychological thriller, all my favorites and quite sensational.<br/><br/>It is 1953 in Stalin's Russia and there's a serial killer on the loose. Only that cannot be; Stalin's Russia boasts of being crime free. Therefore, when Leo of the MGB (later known as the KGB) is sent to a colleague's home to investigate the murder of his son, it is not to investigate at all but to sweep it</span>
  <span id="freeText2268569642997657477" style="display:none">Three words: FAB - U - LOUS! So much to love in this multi-genre book--part historical fiction and cultural; part murder mystery/psychological thriller, all my favorites and quite sensational.<br /><br />It is 1953 in Stalin's Russia and there's a serial killer on the loose. Only that cannot be; Stalin's Russia boasts of being crime free. Therefore, when Leo of the MGB (later known as the KGB) is sent to a colleague's home to investigate the murder of his son, it is not to investigate at all but to sweep it under the rug, to convince the family it was an accidental death. Still, despite not admitting to any real crimes existing, the MGB fabricates all sorts of accusations of spying by ordinary citizens (one of which is Leo's wife), looking at someone the wrong way, etc., and will go to great efforts to prove these innocent people guilty. Everyone is guilty until proven innocent, but no one is ever proven innocent and consequences are paid in big ways. Trust and hope are major themes.<br /><br />There's a lot of background info and setting up of the plot before the action picks up in the latter half of the book. Have patience. It is going to get oh, so good! Leo became a character I could grow to love as he realized what a farce his job and life were, and that there really was someone out there murdering young people. And as his underground, unauthorized investigation will reveal, 44 known murders fit the killer's modus operandi.<br /><br />Who to trust turns out to be NO ONE. Even friends and family have ulterior motives for their actions. A great depiction of life, really survival, in the USSR in those times, with many twists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2268569642997657477" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1438105856" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1438105856" />
    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1438105856?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1438105856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15172229072242712738">2.5, to be precise.<br/><br/>I don't know if, historically, "Child 44" is 100% accurate. Actually, I think it's far from it. I do know one thing though: it is one hell of a page-turner. I'd like to make clear that as far as I am concerned, being a page-turner doesn't make a book good. I mean Dan Brown's "Inferno" was supposedly a page-turner, but, in my humble opinion, it actually was far from good, making it really hard to turn the pages... There are a bunch of other requirements that need to be met in o</span>
  <span id="freeText15172229072242712738" style="display:none">2.5, to be precise.<br /><br />I don't know if, historically, "Child 44" is 100% accurate. Actually, I think it's far from it. I do know one thing though: it is one hell of a page-turner. I'd like to make clear that as far as I am concerned, being a page-turner doesn't make a book good. I mean Dan Brown's "Inferno" was supposedly a page-turner, but, in my humble opinion, it actually was far from good, making it really hard to turn the pages... There are a bunch of other requirements that need to be met in order for me to say a book is good. "Child 44" is good. Let me explain.<br /><br />It is a book with a lot going on. Smith goes in deep and manages not only to come back alive and in one piece, but also with the golden coin in his hand (I think I just made up an expression!). Consider this story as a bunch of threads, all mixed up forming a ball. Now each thread represents an element of the story, and they are quite a few. One of the main challenges here is to weave the story tightly and in a make-sense order. The guy simply nailed it. So where did these two and a half stars go?<br /><br />First and foremost, it's that "Child 44" has a few of these modern literature cliches, which I'm honestly starting to get tired of. Secondly, I didn't like the ending very much. Up to the last 30 pages or so, we have this great story, an exceptional development and one hell of a climax. And then something happens and I'm left wondering. I felt like everything was too convenient in the end. Like everything served the purpose of creating a series. Sorry, that didn't work for me. <br /><br />In conclusion, other than those two flaws, "Child 44" is a good book that just missed the opportunity of being great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15172229072242712738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n2VmMreGNEk1xey0mAE029461XFrg9BJfkMuRGgXcdalQw/gDNj9IZLuWNVv9D4ECGUyuaUOXi4lPWj1pVSDTA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ldz69oVAthoyiS2b37eaTwXBsBkHKtlGQD9QiBIi/gsX+pMkPh5/cpWimfooQpCQ055X0cmnVyEbQRY532EMkQ==')})
  }

Ban đầu, ông ta thuộc phe đối địch với Julius Caesar nhưng về sau đã quy hàng và được Caesar trọng dụng. Có điều, cuối cùng ông lại chính là kẻ đã ám sát thành công Ceasar dẫn đến sự sụp đổ của Đế chế La Mã. Vậy trong câu truyện về trái tim kẻ phản bội này, Higashino Keigo muốn truyền thông điệp gì tới người đọc?<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1431022956807479556\" href=\"#\" <span id="freeTextContainer12390287493756167155">Tom Rob Smith (born 1979) is an English writer. The son of a Swedish mother and an English father, Smith was raised in London where he lives today. After graduating from Cambridge University in 2001, he completed his studies in Italy, studying creative writing for a year. After these studies, he worked as a scriptwriter.<br/><br/>His first novel, Child 44, about a series of child murders in Stalinist Russia</span>
  <span id="freeText12390287493756167155" style="display:none">Tom Rob Smith (born 1979) is an English writer. The son of a Swedish mother and an English father, Smith was raised in London where he lives today. After graduating from Cambridge University in 2001, he completed his studies in Italy, studying creative writing for a year. After these studies, he worked as a scriptwriter.<br /><br />His first novel, Child 44, about a series of child murders in Stalinist Russia, appeared in early 2008 and was translated into 17 languages. It was awarded the 2008 Ian Fleming Steel Dagger for Best Thriller of the year by the Crime Writer's Association. It was recently a Barnes &amp; Noble recommended book. On July 29, 2008 the book was named on the long list for the 2008 Man Booker Prize. In November 2008, he was nominated for the 2008 Costa First Novel Award (former Whitbread).<br /><br />Child 44 followed-up by The Secret Speech (2009)and Agent 6 (2011).<br /><br />Japanese: トム ロブ スミス </span>
